We need to see if Bazza can systematically force the sum to exceed λ n for some odd n. Similarly, Alice can try to force the sum of squares to exceed n for some even n.

This looks like a "game of inequalities" where each player's move adds to both sums, and the constraints are on the cumulative sums after their own moves. The game can be seen as a kind of "alternating" game where each player tries to keep the other's resource within bounds, but also can increase their own resource to eventually force a violation.

We can attempt to characterize the set of sequences that satisfy both constraints for all n. That is, sequences x_n ≥ 0 such that for all odd n, sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n, and for all even n, sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. Since the game continues indefinitely if both players can always choose numbers that keep these inequalities satisfied. The question is: can one player force that eventually the other cannot move? That is, can one player ensure that the sequence of moves is forced into a violation of the other's constraint? That's like a "strategy stealing" or "pairing strategy" game.

Alternatively, we can consider the game as a "two-player" game where each turn the player chooses a number, and the state is (S, Q, n). The players want to force that at some turn, the condition for the opponent fails.

Let’s analyze more generally.

Let’s denote the state at the start of a turn (before the move) as (S, Q, n). We consider the turn number n.

If n is odd (Alice's turn), she chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S + x_n ≤ λ n. She can choose any x_n in [0, λ n - S]. She wants to make Q + x_n^2 as large as possible to eventually cause Bazza's constraint to be violated. But she also must consider that her move adds to Q, which might help her win later. However, she must also ensure that she can keep playing; if she chooses too large x_n, the sum S may exceed λ n? No, she chooses x_n such that the sum after her move is ≤ λ n. So she can choose up to λ n - S. But she also might want to avoid that on her future turns, the sum constraint becomes too restrictive. So she needs to plan.

Similarly, Bazza on even turns chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q + x_n^2 ≤ n. He wants to make S + x_n as large as possible to cause Alice's sum constraint to be violated.

Thus the game is a kind of "alternating" game where each player can increase the other's resource, and they have to keep their own resource within bounds.

Thus we need to analyze the game more systematically. This is a typical "alternating game with constraints" that can be solved by considering "potential" or "critical" values.

Define the state after each turn (including the move) as (S, Q, n). But the constraints are only applied after the move. The game continues if at the start of each turn, the condition for that player to move is satisfied. Since players can always choose 0 if the condition holds, the game will continue indefinitely if both players can always choose numbers that keep the constraints satisfied, but they might also try to force a violation.

One approach: Consider the "worst-case" for Alice: she wants to force a win. She can try to make Q_n exceed n for some even n. She can control Q on her turns. Bazza will try to make S_n exceed λ n for some odd n. So the game is like two "resources" that each player can increase.

We can think of the game as a "two-player" game where each player has a "budget" that increases linearly with n: for Alice, the sum budget is λ n; for Bazza, the square budget is n. The players can spend their budget to increase the cumulative sum or squares, but they also add to the opponent's cumulative sum or squares.

Alternatively, we can think of the game in terms of "increments". Let’s denote the cumulative sum after each turn:

Let s_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

We have constraints:
- If n is odd: s_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even: q_n ≤ n.

We need to analyze the possible sequences that satisfy these constraints for all n. If there is a strategy for Alice to force a violation of Bazza's constraint, then she wins. Similarly for Bazza.

Observation: The constraints are "alternating". If we consider the sequence of "potential" values like s_n - λ n, q_n - n, but they must be ≤0 at the respective parity.

Another idea: Consider the "sum of squares" constraint for even n. Since Alice moves on odd n, she can increase q by choosing x_n. But she must keep s_n within λ n. She might try to make q_n as large as possible quickly. Similarly, Bazza on even n tries to make s_n as large as possible.

We can try to derive necessary and sufficient conditions for a sequence to exist such that both constraints hold for all n. That is, the "survival" sequences. Then the players can try to force the opponent into a situation where the sequence cannot be extended. This is similar to the "game of two constraints" where the players can choose numbers and the state is (s, q, n). We can try to find a "strategy" for one player to always keep the opponent within bounds, i.e., to avoid losing. If a player can force the opponent to eventually exceed the bound, then that player wins.

Let’s try to analyze the game in terms of "critical" values. Suppose Alice wants to win. She can try to make q_n > n for some even n. Since she controls the odd moves, she can choose numbers that increase q. But each time she increases q, she also increases s. The sum constraint on her moves limits how much she can increase s. So she needs to plan a sequence that, after some number of her moves, q becomes too large for the next even turn, regardless of Bazza's responses.

Bazza will try to prevent that by, on his even moves, choosing numbers that increase s, making it harder for her to later increase s enough to cause a large q? Actually, Bazza's choice of x_n adds to s and to q. If he chooses a large number, then q increases, which is bad for him? He wants to avoid q exceeding n for his own constraint. So he will choose numbers that keep q within bounds. He might choose small numbers to keep q low, but then s is low, which makes it easier for Alice to later increase s? Actually, if he chooses small numbers, then s is small, so Alice can choose larger numbers on her next turn to increase q. But she also must keep s within λ n. So if s is small, she has more room to choose large x_n. So to prevent Alice from making q too large, Bazza might want to increase s to the maximum allowed? But then he must also respect his own q constraint. He can increase s by choosing large x_n, but then q increases, which might make him lose later. So he must balance.

We need to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that at some point, the sum of squares before her turn is already too high that she can force a win, or perhaps she can force Bazza to exceed his square constraint.

This problem seems like a known "inequality game" with two constraints. I've seen a similar problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game. On odd turns, Alice chooses a nonnegative number x_n such that the sum of all numbers chosen so far is at most nλ. So Bazza wins at turn 103.

Thus for λ=0.9, Bazza can force a win. In our simulation, we assumed that Bazza always chooses the maximum allowed (subject to his square constraint) to increase S, and Alice always chooses the maximum allowed (subject to her sum constraint) to survive. But we saw that she could have chosen smaller numbers to keep S low. However, in the simulation, she was forced to choose x3 = 3λ - S2, which is the maximum she could choose, because she wanted to increase Q. But maybe she could choose a smaller x3 to keep S lower, and then later she could choose a larger x5? Actually, her goal is to win; she might want to keep S low to survive longer. In the simulation, if she had chosen x3 = 0.5 instead of 1.2858, then S3 would be smaller, and then Bazza's subsequent choices could be larger? Actually, if she chooses smaller x_n, the sum S is smaller, which makes it easier for Bazza to increase S later? But also she wants to make Q large enough to cause Bazza's violation. If she chooses smaller x3, Q3 is smaller, making it harder for her to later force a violation. So there is a trade-off. The optimal strategy for Alice might be to choose x_n to maximize her chances of winning, possibly by using a "critical" sequence that is like "she chooses x_n such that the sum S after her move is exactly λ n, i.e., she uses the full budget each turn." That is what we did in the simulation. But we also saw that for λ=1.2, with a=0, she won on turn 3. That suggests that for λ > 1, she can win by a simple strategy. For λ=1, we saw that she can win by a=0. So perhaps for λ ≥ 1, she has a winning strategy. For λ < 1, Bazza can win.

But we need to test λ=0.9 more carefully. Is there a strategy for Alice that could win? Possibly by choosing a different x1, not 0. For λ=0.9, she could choose x1=0.9 (max). Then S1=0.9, Q1=0.81. Turn 2: Bazza must choose x2 with x2^2 ≤ 2 - 0.81 = 1.19, so x2 ≤ 1.091. He wants to maximize S, so he chooses x2=1.091. Then S2=0.9+1.091=1.991, Q2=0.81+1.19=2.0.
